Hadd for Intercourse with a Relative's Slave-Girl: Shubha in Subject vs. Act
Ruling with proof on hadd for relative's slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubha fi al-mahall (doubt in the subject) and shubha fi al-fil (doubt in the act) and apply the Hanafi rule that only shubha fi al-fil prevents the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Ownership-based doubt never prevents hadd; only act-based doubt (e.g., false marriage) does.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi hudud law: shubha (doubt) regarding the lawfulness of the subject (the person) does not avert the hadd penalty, but shubha regarding the act itself (e.g., believing she is your wife) can. By examining the case of a man who has intercourse with his brother's female slave, we learn why the claim of 'I thought it was allowed' is rejected. We also see exceptions where hadd is dropped and lineage established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The passage starts by stating that hadd (the fixed penalty for zina) is wajib for a man who has intercourse with his brother's female slave, his uncle's, or any relative except direct ancestors and descendants. The default: hadd applies.
This is a clear ruling; the commentary will then justify why.
2
Understand the rationale: no shubha in the subject
The commentary explains that there is no evidence (dalil) indicating that the female slave of a relative is lawful for the man. Ownership belongs to the relative, not to him. Thus, there is no shubha fi al-mahall (doubt in the subject). The man's claim that he assumed it was lawful is rejected because the legal basis for lawfulness is absent.
Shubha fi al-mahall requires some recognized ground for doubt, e.g., the woman being his wife or his own slave.
3
Distinguish from shubha in the act
The passage then lists exceptions where hadd is not imposed, such as a woman presented to him as his wife (false wife). In that case, there is shubha fi al-fil (doubt in the act) because he reasonably believed the marriage was valid. This doubt prevents the hadd.
The distinction is crucial: shubha in the subject (ownership) never prevents hadd; shubha in the act (mistaken belief about the situation) can.
4
Note the correction of an error from al-Nihayah
The passage corrects a claim in al-Nihayah that lineage is established by intercourse with a grandson's slave. The correct Hanafi view is that it is not, because the grandfather does not own that slave during the father's lifetime. This shows the importance of precise ownership analysis.
This is an intra-Hanafi correction; al-Nihayah's view is considered a mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود الوطء الذي يوجب الحد
وحد بوطء أمة أخيه، وعمه، وأجنبية وجدها على فراشه
(وحد بوطء أمة أخيه ، وعمه ) ، وأجنبية) وجدها على فراشه
ابنه ، فإنّه لا يثبت به النسب وإن ادّعاه ؛ لأنّ الجد لا يتملكها حال حياة الأب، نعم إن صدقه ابنُ الابن عُتِقَ لزعمه أنه عمه، وما في «النهاية» من أنه يثبت نسبه في هذه الصورة أيضاً فغلط ، كذا في «فتح القدير وكذا يستثنى من صور شبهة الفعل بالمطلّقة ثلاثاً، فإنّه يثبت النسب بوطئها بالشرط المذكور في موضعه، وهو أن تلد لأقل من سنتين ، وبعد سنتين أيضاً بالدعوة ، وكذا المختلعة والمطلقة بعوض، كذا في «النهاية».
ويستثنى أيضاً وطء من زفت إليه، وقال النساء : هي زوجتك، ولم تكن كذلك، فإنّه يثبت النسب فيه إذا ادّعاه ، وهذا إذا جعلت هذه الصورة من صور الشبهة في الفعل، كذا في «البحر». [١] قوله : بوطء أمة أخيه وعمه ؛ وكذا وطء أمةٍ غير الأصول والفروع، كالخالِ والعمة والخالة وأولادهم ؛ وذلك لأنه ليس هنا دليل يدلُّ على حل المحل، فلم توجد شبهة في المحل، وليس بينه وبين أقاربه غير الأصول والفروع كمالُ انبساط واختلاط ، بحيث يورث شبهة في الفعل، فدعوى ظنّه الحلّ لا تعتبر، نعم إن قال : لم أعلم أن الزني حرامٌ لم يحدّ. كذا في «الفتح»)
Book of Hudud: Intercourse that necessitates the hadd penalty. And he (the author) said: Hadd is imposed for intercourse with the female slave of his brother, his uncle, and an unrelated woman that he found on his bed. (Commentary: The text says: (and hadd for intercourse with the female slave of his brother, his uncle), and an unrelated woman that he found on his bed. Regarding his grandson, lineage is not established by it even if he claims it, because the grandfather does not own her during the father's lifetime. Yes, if the son of the son confirms him, she is freed due to his assumption that he is his uncle. What is in al-Nihayah that lineage is established in this case too is an error. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Also excluded from cases of shubha in the act is intercourse with a triple-divorced woman: lineage is established by her intercourse under the condition mentioned in its place, which is that she gives birth in less than two years, and after two years also by claim. Likewise the khul' woman and the woman divorced for compensation. Thus in al-Nihayah. Also excluded is intercourse with a woman who is escorted to him and the women say: 'She is your wife,' but she is not, for lineage is established because of his claim. This is if this case is considered a case of shubha in the act. Thus in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'for intercourse with the female slave of his brother and his uncle' includes likewise intercourse with a female slave of relatives other than ascendants and descendants, such as maternal uncle, paternal aunt, maternal aunt, and their children; this is because there is no evidence here indicating the lawfulness of the subject, so there is no shubha in the subject, and between him and his relatives other than ascendants and descendants there is not complete intimacy and mixing such that would create shubha in the act. So the claim of his assumption of lawfulness is not considered. Yes, if he says: 'I did not know that fornication is forbidden,' he is not flogged. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It states the hadd penalty (flogging) for a man who has intercourse with the female slave of his brother or uncle, or any relative except direct ascendants (mother, grandmother) and descendants (daughter, granddaughter). The reasoning is that these slaves are owned by the relative, not by the man, so there is no shubha (doubt) regarding the lawfulness of the subject (the woman). Furthermore, close intimacy and mixing between the man and these relatives are not such that he could confuse the slave for his own. Therefore, the claim that he thought it was lawful is not accepted. However, an exception is made for the case of a woman presented as his wife (by the women's false statement), which constitutes shubha in the act, and the hadd is not imposed for that. The passage also clarifies that lineage is not established by intercourse with a grandson's slave, since the grandfather does not own her during the father's lifetime. This is a correct ruling in the Hanafi madhhab, contrary to what al-Nihayah states.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity in the lawfulness of intercourse that prevents the hadd penalty.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by someone other than the fornicator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a man who has intercourse with his brother's female slave receive the hadd penalty?
A) Because the brother's slave is never lawful under any circumstances.
B) Because there is no shubha in the subject or act to create doubt about lawfulness.
C) Because he must obtain written permission from his brother.
D) Because the slave is a mahram.

Key Takeaway
Shubha in the subject (ownership) never prevents hadd; only shubha in the act (reasonable mistaken belief) can.

Hadd for Finding a Woman on His Bed: Shubha and Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling with proof and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when a man finds a woman on his bed, identify the conditions for shubha (doubt), and explain the relied-upon opinion differentiating night and day.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted only when a plausible shubha exists, such as darkness or deception.

The hadd penalty for unlawful intercourse is severe, but the Hanafi school recognizes certain situations where doubt (shubha) prevents its application. This passage from al-Hidayah, expanded by later commentaries, addresses a specific scenario: a man finds a woman on his bed and assumes she is his wife. The ruling depends on whether the circumstances provide a valid excuse. We will unpack the primary ruling, the conditions for shubha, and the intra-madhhab disagreement that settles the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that the act is intercourse with an unrelated woman, which is zina. The default ruling is that hadd (penalty) is imposed. The passage from al-Hidayah states this clearly: 'Whoever finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her bears the hadd penalty.'
The default is based on the absence of valid marriage or ownership.
2
Locate the proof (talil) for the ruling
The proof is not a Quranic verse or hadith but a rational argument: after prolonged companionship with his wife, he should be able to distinguish; merely assuming she is his wife is not based on evidence. The text says: 'because there is no confusion after prolonged companionship, so his assumption is not based on evidence.' This is a talil (suitability-based reasoning).
The absence of shubha makes the act unequivocally zina.
3
Identify the exceptions (shubha)
The al-Hidayah text mentions one exception: if he called his wife and an unrelated woman answered, claiming to be his wife, then her statement is evidence (shubha), so no hadd. The commentator then expands by noting other views: at night, darkness creates shubha; in daytime, no shubha unless deception occurs.
The key is whether the man had a reasonable basis for his mistake.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are three positions: (1) Abu Yusuf and Ya'qub (one narration from Abu Hanifa) hold hadd applies always. (2) Zufar (another narration from Abu Hanifa) distinguishes night vs. day: no hadd at night, hadd during the day. (3) The commentary notes that the relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi) is Zufar's view, as preferred by Abu al-Layth in al-Hawi.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether hadd is executed in night-time cases.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
The mufta bihi is the narration of Zufar: no hadd at night (due to darkness), hadd during the day. The rationale: darkness provides a genuine excuse, while daytime gives the opportunity to verify. The woman's response when called also establishes shubha.
This position balances the severity of hadd with human fallibility.

Source Text

[٢] قوله : وأجنبيّة ؛ عطف على قوله : «أمة أخيه»، قال في «الهداية»: «مَن وجد امرأة على فراشه فوطئها فعليه الحد ؛ لأنه لا اشتباه بعد طول الصحبة، فلم يكن الظنّ مستنداً إلى دليل، وهذا لأنه قد ينام على فراشه غيرها من المحارم التي في بيتها. وكذا إذا كان أعمى ؛ لأنه يمكنه التمييز بالسؤال وغيره، إلا إذا كان دعاها فأجابته أجنبية وقالت : أنا زوجتك فواقعها ؛ لأنّ الإخبار «دليل». انتهى [٣]قوله : وجدها ؛ الجملة صفة لأجنبية، وإطلاقه مشير إلى أن الحكم متّحد فيما إذا كان ذلك ليلاً وفيما إذا كان ذلك نهاراً ، وهذا هو الذي عليه المتون وأكثر الشروح.
وعند أبي يوسف الله يحدون جميعاً.
وفي «الظهيرية»: رجلٌ وجد في بيته امرأة في ليلة ظلماء فغشيها، وقال: ظننتُ أنها امرأتى لا حدّ عليه، ولو كان نهاراً يحد.

[2] His statement: 'and an unrelated woman' is conjoined to his statement 'female slave of his brother.' He said in al-Hidayah: 'Whoever finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her bears the hadd penalty because there is no confusion after prolonged companionship, so his assumption is not based on evidence. This is because other female relatives who he could have in his house might sleep on his bed. Likewise if he is blind, because he can distinguish by asking etc., unless he called her and an unrelated woman answered, saying "I am your wife" and he had intercourse with her, because the report is evidence.' [3] His statement: 'whom he found' is an adjective for 'unrelated woman,' and its absoluteness indicates that the ruling is the same whether it is night or day, and this is what the texts and most commentaries follow. And according to Abu Yusuf, all are flogged. In al-Zhahiriyyah: A man found a woman in his house on a dark night and had intercourse with her, saying 'I thought she was my wife' – no hadd on him, but if it was daytime he would be flogged. In al-Hawi: from Zufar from Abu Hanifa: Whoever finds a woman in his wedding bower or house and says 'I thought she was my wife' – if it was daytime he is flogged, if night he is not flogged. From Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa: He bears the hadd whether night or day. Abu al-Layth said: We adopt the narration of Zufar.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a man who finds an unrelated woman on his bed and has intercourse with her. The primary ruling from al-Hidayah is that he is subject to hadd because after extended companionship with his wife, he should be able to distinguish; his assumption that she is his wife is not based on evidence. However, if he called his wife and an unrelated woman responded, claiming to be his wife, then that constitutes shubha and hadd is not imposed. The commentary notes disagreement among Hanafi scholars: some (like al-Zhahiriyyah and a narration from Zufar) distinguish between night and day – if it is a dark night, his claim of mistaking her for his wife is accepted; if daytime, not. Abu Yusuf says hadd applies in all cases. A different view from Abu Hanifa (via Ya'qub) also imposes hadd in all cases. The relied-upon position (according to Abu al-Layth) is the narration of Zufar, which differentiates between night and day.

Key Terms
فراش (firash): The bed or sleeping place; finding a woman on one's bed implies a possible mistake.
شبهة في الفعل (shubha fi al-fi'l): Doubt in the act itself, here due to deception or darkness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi madhhab, when is hadd not imposed on a man who finds a woman on his bed?
A) If he is blind
B) If he calls his wife and an unrelated woman answers, claiming to be his wife
C) If it is daytime
D) Always

Key Takeaway
Hadd is averted only when a plausible shubha exists, such as darkness at night or an affirmative response from the woman; the mere claim of mistaken identity is insufficient.

Hadd for Dhimmi and Harbi Intercourse: Status of Musta'min
Ruling on hadd for zina between dhimmi and harbi musta'min in Dar al-Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when hadd applies to a dhimmi and a harbi musta'min in cases of zina in Dar al-Islam and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the night/day distinction.

Core Idea
A dhimmi is subject to hadd; a harbi musta'min is not; nighttime can create doubt that precludes hadd.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary clarifies a nuanced corner of Islamic criminal law: the application of hadd when a non-Muslim is not under full subjection to Islamic penal law. The text distinguishes between dhimmis (permanent residents under covenant) and harbi musta'min (temporary visitors with safe conduct). It also records a difference within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of night versus day on the claim of mistaken identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and locations
Distinguish between dhimmi (resident non-Muslim) and harbi (enemy non-Muslim with aman). Also note whether the act occurs in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb.
Dar al-Harb: no hadd at all by Hanafi consensus.
2
State the general rule for Dar al-Islam
In Dar al-Islam, dhimmis are subject to hadd for zina, like Muslims. Harbi musta'min are not subject to hadd because they lack permanent status under Islamic jurisdiction.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa as presented in the text.
3
Apply to the four possible scenarios
Harbi with dhimmiyya → only dhimmiyya flogged. Dhimmi with harbiyya → only dhimmi flogged. Both harbi → no hadd. Both dhimmi → both flogged.
The text emphasizes that the hadd is assigned only to the dhimmi/dhimmiyya.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on night vs day
The text records two narrations from Abu Hanifa: via Zufar (no hadd at night due to doubt) and via Ya'qub (hadd even at night). The mufta bihi follows Zufar.
The principle: doubt (shubha) precludes hadd; nighttime can create such doubt.

وفي «الظهيرية»: رجلٌ وجد في بيته امرأة في ليلة ظلماء فغشيها، وقال: ظننتُ أنها امرأتى لا حدّ عليه، ولو كان نهاراً يحد.

[١]قوله : وذمية ؛ يعني إذا زنى حربي مستأمن دخل دارنـا بأمان بذمية حدت
[٢] قوله : وذمي ؛ يعني حدّ ذمي زنى بحربية مستأمنة دخلت دارنا بأمان. [٣] قوله : لا الحربي والحربية؛ أي لا يُحدُّ الحربي الزاني في الصورة الأولى، ولا الحربية المزنية في الصورة الثانية، ويمكن أن يكون معناه أنه إذا زنى الحربي المستأمن بالحربية المستأمنة لا يُحدّان ، وعدم حد الحربي والحربية في الصورتين السابقتين فهم من تخصيص إسنادِ الحد إلى الذمي ،والذميّة، والحاصل: إنّه لا حدّ على الحربي والحربية المستأمنين عند أبي حنيفة
[4] His statement: 'and that' is an explanation of why the harbi and harbiyya are intended as musta'min (those granted aman). The summary is that this is intended because if this – i.e., the harbi's intercourse with a dhimmiyya, and the dhimmi's intercourse with a harbiyya – occurs in Dar al-Harb, the hadd is not wajib by consensus of our imams, because fornication in Dar al-Harb does not necessitate the hadd, even if the fornicator and fornicatress are Muslims, as will be mentioned later, God willing. In al-Zhahiriyyah: A man found a woman in his house on a dark night and had intercourse with her, saying 'I thought she was my wife' – no hadd on him, but if it was daytime he would be flogged. In al-Hawi: from Zufar from Abu Hanifa: Whoever finds a woman in his wedding bower or house and says 'I thought she was my wife' – if it was daytime he is flogged, if night he is not flogged. From Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa: He bears the hadd whether night or day. Abu al-Layth said: We adopt the narration of Zufar. Regarding the fornicatress. [1] His statement: 'and a dhimmiyya' means if a harbi musta'min who enters our land with aman fornicates with a dhimmiyya, she is flogged. [2] His statement: 'and a dhimmi' means that a dhimmi who fornicates with a harbiyya musta'mina who entered our land with aman is flogged. [3] His statement: 'not the harbi and the harbiyya' means the harbi fornicator in the first case is not flogged, nor the harbiyya in the second case. It could also mean that if a harbi musta'min fornicates with a harbiyya musta'mina, both are not flogged. The absence of hadd for the harbi and harbiyya in the two preceding cases is understood from the specification of assigning the hadd to the dhimmi and dhimmiyya. The conclusion: There is no hadd on the harbi or harbiyya musta'min according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on hadd when a harbi (enemy non-Muslim with aman) or dhimmi (resident non-Muslim) commits zina with each other's women. The key point: if the intercourse occurs in Dar al-Harb, no hadd applies by agreement of the Hanafi imams, because the rules of Islamic penalties are not enforced in enemy territory. But in Dar al-Islam, a harbi who fornicates with a dhimmiyya is not flogged (since he is not subject to Islamic law), but the dhimmiyya is flogged. Similarly, a dhimmi who fornicates with a harbiyya is flogged. If both are harbis (musta'min), neither is flogged according to Abu Hanifa. This is because the hadd punishment is only applicable to those who are subject to Islamic criminal law: dhimmis and Muslims are subject; harbis who have aman are not subject to hudud penalties for acts within Dar al-Islam (except for certain violations like breach of peace).

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): An enemy non-Muslim who is not under Muslim rule nor has a covenant; if granted aman (safe conduct), he becomes a musta'min.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a covenant (dhimma), subject to Islamic laws except for specific religious exemptions.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim granted temporary safe passage (aman) into Muslim territory; retains personal status and is not subject to Islamic penal law for hudud.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or mistaken belief that can prevent the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a harbi musta'min fornicates with a dhimmiyya in Dar al-Islam, who is flogged?
A) Both are flogged
B) Neither is flogged
C) Only the dhimmiyya is flogged
D) Only the harbi is flogged

Key Takeaway
A harbi musta'min is not subject to hadd for zina in Dar al-Islam, while a dhimmi is; and valid doubt (like nighttime) can prevent hadd even for those normally subject.

Ruling on Zina under Mistaken Identity at Night vs Day
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd waiver for mistaken identity at night vs. day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi on hadd for zina committed under mistaken identity at night vs. day and identify the conflicting narrations from Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

Core Idea
At night, mistaken identity may waive hadd; by day, it does not.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of hadd when a man commits zina with a woman he mistakes for his wife. The key factor is the time of day: does darkness create a valid excuse (shubha) that prevents punishment? Two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa provide opposite rulings, and later authorities preferred one over the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the case: a man finds a woman in his house at night (dark) or during the day and has intercourse, claiming he thought she was his wife.
The key variable is night vs. day.
2
State the ruling from al-Zahiriyya and Zufar
According to al-Zahiriyya and Zufar's narration from Abu Hanifa, no hadd at night; hadd applies during the day.
This is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
3
Contrast with Ya'qub's narration
Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) narrates from Abu Hanifa that hadd applies regardless of day or night.
This is a dissenting opinion within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the rationale
The basis for waiving hadd at night is the plausible mistake due to darkness, which constitutes shubha. By day, the mistake is less plausible, so hadd is imposed.
Shubha is a key concept in hudud.
5
Identify the tarjih
Abu al-Layth states: 'We take the narration of Zufar.' Therefore the operative ruling is no hadd at night.
This is the final fatwa position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «الظهيرية»: رجلٌ وجد في بيته امرأة في ليلة ظلماء فغشيها، وقال: ظننتُ أنها امرأتى لا حدّ عليه، ولو كان نهاراً يحد. وفي «الحاوي»: عن زفر الله عن أبي حنيفة الله : فيمن وجدَ في حَجْلَته) أو بيته امرأة فقال : ظننتُ أنها امرأتى إن كان نهاراً يحد، وإن كان ليلاً لا يحد، وعن يعقوب عن أبي حنيفة : إن عليه الحد ليلاً كان أو نهاراً ، قال أبو الليث : وبرواية زفر نأخذ
And in al-Zahiriyya: A man found a woman in his house on a dark night and had intercourse with her, and said: 'I thought she was my wife.' There is no hadd on him. But if it were daytime, he would be hadd. And in al-Hawi: From Zufar, from Abu Hanifa: Regarding one who found a woman in his enclosures or his house and said, 'I thought she was my wife' — if it was daytime, he is hadd; if night, not. And from Ya'qub, from Abu Hanifa: He is hadd whether night or day. Abu al-Layth said: We take the narration of Zufar.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents multiple narrations within the Hanafi school on the ruling of hadd when a man commits zina with a woman he mistakes for his wife. The key factor is darkness (night) vs. daylight. According to al-Zahiriyya and the narration of Zufar from Abu Hanifa, at night no hadd due to the possibility of mistaken identity, but during the day, the excuse is not accepted, and hadd applies. However, Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) narrates from Abu Hanifa that hadd applies in both cases. Abu al-Layth chooses the narration of Zufar as the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A valid excuse or doubt that prevents the application of hadd.
حَجْلَة (hajlah): An enclosure or bridal chamber decorated with curtains and beds, as explained in the commentary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on hadd for a man who commits zina with a woman he mistakes for his wife at night?
A) Hadd applies.
B) No hadd.
C) It depends on whether the woman was a dhimmi.
D) He must pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
Darkness at night is a valid shubha that waives hadd for mistaken identity zina; by day, hadd is imposed.

Hadd for Harbi and Dhimmi in Zina
States matn ruling on hadd for harbi/dhimmi with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for a harbi musta'min and a dhimmi in cases of zina, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A harbi musta'min is not subject to hadd for zina because full Islamic jurisdiction is lacking.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling in hudud: whether a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) who enters Muslim territory with aman (safe conduct) is liable for hadd if he commits zina with a dhimmi woman. The matn clarifies that the dhimmi is subject to hadd, but the harbi is not. The commentary further explains that this applies only when the act occurs within Islamic jurisdiction, with an important intra-Hanafi difference noted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the terms: harbi (enemy combatant), dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), and musta'min (temporary resident with aman). The passage deals with a harbi musta'min and a dhimmi.
The harbi is an unbeliever from dar al-harb; the dhimmi is a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In the first scenario (harbi musta'min with dhimmi woman), the dhimmi woman is hadd, but the harbi is not. In the second scenario (dhimmi with harbiyya musta'mina), the dhimmi is hadd, but the harbiyya is not.
The matn explicitly attributes hadd only to the dhimmi/dhimmiyya.
3
Note the exception of dar al-harb
If the zina occurred in dar al-harb, hadd is not obligatory by consensus, even if the parties are Muslims.
This is mentioned in the commentary to clarify that the ruling applies only within Islamic jurisdiction.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that all parties (including the harbi musta'min) are subject to hadd, because the musta'min has agreed to abide by Islamic laws during his stay.
Abu Hanifa restricts this agreement to mu'amalat, not 'uqubat.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: no hadd on the harbi musta'min.
This is the standard view found in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

[١]قوله : وذمية ؛ يعني إذا زنى حربي مستأمن دخل دارنا بأمان بذمية حدت [٢] قوله : وذمي ؛ يعني حد ذمي زنى بحربية مستأمنة دخلت دارنا بأمان. And if he is blind, and a dhimmi woman with whom a harbi commits zina, and a dhimmi who commits zina with a harbiyya, not the harbi and the harbiyya: those entering our land with aman; and that because if this (zina) occurs in dar al-harb, hadd is not obligatory. And according to Abu Yusuf, all are hadd. [1] His saying: 'and a dhimmi woman': means if a harbi musta'min enters our land with aman and commits zina with a dhimmi, she is hadd. [2] His saying: 'and a dhimmi': means a dhimmi is hadd if he commits zina with a harbiyya musta'mina who entered our land with aman. And it can mean that if the harbi musta'min commits zina with the harbiyya musta'mina, they are not hadd. The absence of hadd for the harbi and harbiyya in the two previous scenarios is understood from the specification of attributing hadd to the dhimmi and the dhimmiyya. The result: there is no hadd on the harbi and harbiyya musta'mins according to Abu Hanifa. [4] His saying: 'and that': explanation of why the harbi and harbiyya are intended as musta'mins. And his saying: 'and a dhimmiyya' is an apposition to the implied pronoun in 'hadd', and this is permissible because of the separator.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on hadd for zina involving a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) and a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim). The matn establishes that no hadd is imposed on the harbi or harbiyya themselves, but hadd is imposed on the dhimmi or dhimmiyya who are the active partners. The commentary clarifies that this applies to musta'mins (temporary residents with aman). If the zina occurred in dar al-harb, hadd is not obligatory by consensus. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that all parties are hadd. The commentary then explains the grammatical construction of the matn.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): An unbeliever from enemy territory (dar al-harb) not under Muslim protection.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizya and is protected.
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who enters Muslim territory with a temporary safe-conduct (aman) for trade or other purposes.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment for specific offenses like zina, qadhf, theft, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No hadd on either.
C) Only the harbi is hadd.
D) Only the dhimmi woman is hadd.

Key Takeaway
Hadd is only imposed on a dhimmi, not on a harbi musta'min, because hudud require full submission to Islamic jurisdiction.

Hadd for Harbi Musta'min: Asl-Taba' vs Tamkin
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd jurisdiction for temporary residents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on hadd for a harbi musta'min and apply the asl-taba' principle and tamkin as independent causes.

Core Idea
The follower's liability can survive the origin's exemption if her own act (tamkin) independently triggers the hadd.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a harbi (non-Muslim enemy) who enters Muslim lands with temporary safe conduct (musta'min) is subject to hadd for zina. Muhammad al-Shaybani invokes the asl-taba' principle, where the man is the origin and the woman the follower. Abu Hanifa counters by focusing on the woman's act of tamkin (enabling), which itself obligates hadd. The disagreement between these two giants of the school illustrates how legal principles interact with policy considerations of jurisdiction.

Guided Path
1
Map the four scenarios
Identify the four possible combinations: harbi with dhimmi, dhimmi with harbiyya, harbi with harbiyya (implicit), and dhimmi with dhimmi. Determine the ruling in each according to Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.
The text explicitly covers the first two; the last two can be deduced.
2
Understand Muhammad's asl-taba' argument
Analyze why Muhammad treats the man as the origin (asl) and the woman as the follower (taba') in zina. Note how the hadd drops from the follower when it drops from the origin, but not vice versa.
The linguistic point: the woman is called 'mazniyya' not 'zaniya' in the primary sense.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's tamkin response
See how Abu Hanifa argues that the woman's act of tamkin (permitting the act) is a separate cause for hadd, independent of the man's status. Therefore, even if the harbi is not liable, the dhimmi woman can be hadd.
This overrides the asl-taba' presumption when the follower has an independent causative act.
4
Apply the jurisdictional policy
Understand why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad limit the musta'min's liability to rights of people (huquq al-'ibad) and not hudud (rights of God), based on the temporary nature of his stay.
Compare with the dhimmi who has permanent residence and is fully subject.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under the majority view, a harbi musta'min who commits zina is not executed or flogged; under Abu Yusuf's second opinion, he would be.
This affects how authorities treat such cases in dar al-Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وعـنـد محمـد إن زنى الحربي ؛ أي بذمية لا يُحد لا الزاني ، ولا المزنية، وإن زنى الذمي بحربية حد الذمي، وهو قول أبي يوسف له أولاً ، وقوله الآخر هو أنهم يحدون جميعا، ووجهه : إنّ المستأمنَ التزم أحكامنا مـدة مقامه في دارنا في المعاملات، كما أنّ الذمي التزمها مدة عمره ، ومن التزم أحكامنا تجري عليه العقوبات، فيحد الحربي والحربية أيضاً كالذمي ،والذمية، ولهذا يحد الحربي المستأمن حد القذف، ويقتل قصاصاً اتفاقاً. ولأبي حنيفة ومحمد : إن الحربي ما دخل دارنا للقرار، بل لحاجة كالتجارة ونحوها ، فلم يصر من أهل دارنا كالذمي، وإنّما التزم من الأحكام ما يرجع إلى تحصيل مقصوده، وهو ما يتعلق بحقوق العباد والقصاص، وحد القذف من حقوق العباد ، فيؤاخذ به، وأما حد الزنا فهو من حقوق الله فلا يقام عليه، بخلاف الذمي فإنّه التزم القرار في دارنا ، فصار كالمسلم. ثمّ إنّ محمداً لله يقول : إنّ الأصل في باب الزنى هو فعل الرجل، والمرأة تابعة، ولذا يسمّى هو زانياً وواطئاً، وهي موطوءة ومزنيّة، وتسميتها بالزانية مجاز، كالراضية بمعنى المرضية، ففي صورة زنى الحربي بالذميّة لَمَّا سقط الحد عن الأصل لما ذكرنا سقط عن التبع، ولا كذلك العكس، فإنّ امتناع شيءٍ في حق التبع لا يوجب الامتناع في حق الأصل، فيحدّ الذمي الزاني بالحربية، وإن لم تحد الحربية لما ذكرنا. والجواب عنه من قبل أبي حنيفة له : إن فعل الحربي المستأمن زنا بالضرورة، والتمكين من فعل هو زنا موجب للحد، فلما مكنت الذمية نفسها لزنا الحربي وجب عليها الحد بالتمكين، وإن لم يحد الذمي، هذا توضيح ما ذكره صاحب «الهداية» في [٢]قوله : وهذا ... الخ ؛ دفع لما يقال : إنّ العطف على ضمير الرفع المتصل ليس بجائز عند النحاة، فكيف ارتكبه المصنف ، وحاصل الدفع : إن عدم الجواز إنما هو إذا لم توجد الفاصلة بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، وأما عند وجودها فهو جائز.
His saying: 'And according to Muhammad, if the harbi commits zina (with a dhimmi woman), neither the fornicator nor the fornicatress are hadd. And if the dhimmi commits zina with a harbiyya, the dhimmi is hadd, and this is the first opinion of Abu Yusuf, and his other opinion is that all are hadd. And its reasoning: The musta'min has undertaken our laws during his stay in our land in transactions, just as the dhimmi has undertaken them for his lifetime. And whoever undertakes our laws, the penalties apply to him. So the harbi and harbiyya are also hadd like the dhimmi and dhimmiyya. For this reason, the harbi musta'min is subject to hadd for qadhf and is killed in retaliation (qisas) by consensus. And for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: The harbi did not enter our land for permanent residence, but for a need like trade and the like, so he does not become one of the people of our land like the dhimmi. He only undertook the laws that relate to achieving his purpose, which is what pertains to the rights of people and qisas. And hadd for qadhf is from the rights of people, so he is held accountable for it. As for hadd for zina, it is from the rights of God, so it is not imposed on him, unlike the dhimmi, who undertook permanent residence in our land, so he becomes like a Muslim. Then Muhammad says: The origin in the chapter of zina is the act of the man, and the woman is following. So he is called a fornicator and aggressor, and she is the one fornicated with and the object. Calling her a fornicatress is metaphorical, like 'al-radiyya' meaning 'the one who is satisfied'. So in the case of the harbi committing zina with the dhimmiyya, when the hadd dropped from the origin due to what we mentioned, it drops from the follower as well. But not the reverse, for the prevention of something regarding the follower does not necessitate prevention regarding the origin. So the dhimmi fornicator is hadd with the harbiyya, even if the harbiyya is not hadd due to what we mentioned. The response from Abu Hanifa: The act of the harbi musta'min is zina by necessity, and permitting an act that is zina necessitates hadd. So when the dhimmiyya allowed herself to be subjected to zina by the harbi, hadd becomes obligatory on her due to this permission, even if the harbi is not hadd. This is a clarification of what the author of al-Hidayah mentioned. [2] His saying: 'And this...' etc. is a refutation of what is said: that apposition to the attached nominative pronoun is not permissible according to grammarians, so how did the author commit it? The refutation: The impermissibility is only when there is no separator between the appositive and the thing it is in apposition to, but when there is a separator, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (and also Abu Yusuf's two opinions) regarding the liability of a harbi musta'min for hadd for zina. The core issue is whether the musta'min's temporary submission to Islamic law subjects him to all penal laws or only those related to civil rights. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that since the harbi intends only temporary stay, he is not fully under Islamic jurisdiction for hudud which are rights of God. Muhammad further uses the principle of asl (origin) and taba' (follower): the man is the origin in zina, the woman is the follower. So if the origin is not liable (as in the case of the harbi), the follower also is not; but if the follower is not liable, the origin may still be liable. Abu Hanifa responds by focusing on the woman's act of tawkin (permitting the act), which itself is a cause for hadd. The passage ends with a grammatical note about apposition.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The origin or primary actor; in zina, the man is the asl because he actively commits the act.
تبع (taba'): The follower; in zina, the woman is the taba' because she is the passive partner.
تمكين (tamkin): The act of permitting or enabling; here, the woman's allowing the act of zina is a separate cause for hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a harbi musta'min commits zina with a willing dhimmi woman?
A) Both are subject to hadd.
B) Neither is subject to hadd.
C) The harbi is subject to hadd, the dhimmi woman is not.
D) The harbi is not subject to hadd, but the dhimmi woman is.

Key Takeaway
The harbi musta'min is exempt from hadd for zina, but a dhimmi woman who willingly enables the act is liable due to her tamkin, overriding the asl-taba' default.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Hadd for Harbi and Dhimmi in Zina
Intra-madhhab dispute on hadd jurisdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions within the Hanafi school on the application of hadd for zina when a Harbi (musta'min) and a Dhimmi woman are involved, and explain the rationale behind the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A Harbi is not bound by divine penalties; only private laws accepted for his temporary purpose apply.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of the hadd penalty for zina when the parties have different legal statuses. You will work through the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus the two opinions of Abu Yusuf, and see how legal reasoning about temporary residence, subsidiary liability, and the division between huquq Allah and huquq al-ibad shapes the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the scenarios
Identify the two cases: (1) a Harbi (musta'min) commits zina with a Dhimmi woman; (2) a Dhimmi man commits zina with a Harbi woman (Harbiyya). The ruling may differ in each case.
2
Identify the three positions
List the three positions found in the text: (a) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no hadd on either party in case (1); in case (2) the Dhimmi man is hadded but the Harbi woman is not. (b) Abu Yusuf's first opinion: same as (a). (c) Abu Yusuf's second opinion: both parties are hadded in both cases.
Abu Yusuf's second opinion is his later view.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's dalil
Abu Yusuf argues that the musta'min has accepted our laws during his stay, just as the Dhimmi has permanently, so penalties like hadd should apply. He cites analogies: a musta'min is hadded for qadhf and killed in qiṣāṣ by consensus.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's dalil
They argue that the Harbi enters temporarily (e.g., for trade) and only accepts laws related to his purpose (mu'āmalāt, ḥuqūq al-‘ibād). Hadd for zina is a pure right of Allah, not included. Muhammad adds that in zina, the man is the primary agent, the woman subsidiary; when the head's liability falls, the subsidiary's also falls.
5
Evaluate the rebuttal and determine tarjih
Abu Hanifa rebuts the subsidiary argument: the Dhimmi woman's enablement (tamkīn) makes her directly liable, so she could be hadded even if the man is not. However, the madhhab ultimately follows the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (no hadd in the first case) as the zāhir al-riwāyah and muftā bihī.
The later consensus supports this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود الوطء الذي يوجب الحد وعند محمد إن زنى الحربي بالذمية لا يحد. وقوله : وذميَّةٌ عطف على الضمير المستتر في حد، وهذا جائز لوجودِ الفاصلة. [١]قوله : وعـنـد محمـد إن زنى الحربي ؛ أي بذمية لا يُحد لا الزاني ، ولا المزنية، وإن زنى الذمي بحربية حد الذمي، وهو قول أبي يوسف له أولاً ، وقوله الآخر هو أنهم يحدون جميعا، ووجهه : إنّ المستأمنَ التزم أحكامنا مـدة مقامه في دارنا في المعاملات، كما أنّ الذمي التزمها مدة عمره ، ومن التزم أحكامنا تجري عليه العقوبات، فيحد الحربي والحربية أيضاً كالذمي ،والذمية، ولهذا يحد الحربي المستأمن حد القذف، ويقتل قصاصاً اتفاقاً. ولأبي حنيفة ومحمد : إن الحربي ما دخل دارنا للقرار، بل لحاجة كالتجارة ونحوها ، فلم يصر من أهل دارنا كالذمي، وإنّما التزم من الأحكام ما يرجع إلى تحصيل مقصوده، وهو ما يتعلق بحقوق العباد والقصاص، وحد القذف من حقوق العباد ، فيؤاخذ به، وأما حد الزنا فهو من حقوق الله فلا يقام عليه، بخلاف الذمي فإنّه التزم القرار في دارنا ، فصار كالمسلم. ثمّ إنّ محمداً يقول : إنّ الأصل في باب الزنى هو فعل الرجل، والمرأة تابعة، ولذا يسمّى هو زانياً وواطئاً، وهي موطوءة ومزنيّة، وتسميتها بالزانية مجاز، كالراضية بمعنى المرضية، ففي صورة زنى الحربي بالذميّة لَمَّا سقط الحد عن الأصل لما ذكرنا سقط عن التبع، ولا كذلك العكس، فإنّ امتناع شيءٍ في حق التبع لا يوجب الامتناع في حق الأصل، فيحدّ الذمي الزاني بالحربية، وإن لم تحد الحربية لما ذكرنا. والجواب عنه من قبل أبي حنيفة له : إن فعل الحربي المستأمن زنا بالضرورة، والتمكين من فعل هو زنا موجب للحد، فلما مكنت الذمية نفسها لزنا الحربي وجب عليها الحد بالتمكين، وإن لم يحد الذمي، هذا توضيح ما ذكره صاحب «الهداية» في [٢]قوله : وهذا ... الخ ؛ دفع لما يقال : إنّ العطف على ضمير الرفع المتصل ليس بجائز عند النحاة، فكيف ارتكبه المصنف ، وحاصل الدفع : إن عدم الجواز إنما هو إذا لم توجد الفاصلة بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، وأما عند وجودها فهو جائز.
Book of Hudud: Intercourse that necessitates the hadd. According to Muhammad, if a Harbi commits zina with a Dhimmi woman, no hadd is imposed. His statement: 'and a Dhimmi woman' is conjoined to the hidden pronoun in 'حد', and this is permissible due to the existence of a separator. [1] His statement: 'And according to Muhammad, if a Harbi commits zina' i.e., with a Dhimmi woman, no hadd is imposed on the male nor the female. And if a Dhimmi commits zina with a Harbi woman, the Dhimmi is hadded. This is the first opinion of Abu Yusuf. His other opinion is that both are hadded. His basis: The musta'min accepted our laws for the duration of his stay in our land in transactions, just as the Dhimmi accepted them for his lifetime. And whoever accepts our laws, the penalties apply to him. So the Harbi and Harbiya are hadded like the Dhimmi and Dhimmiya. Hence, a musta'min Harbi is hadded for qadhf and killed in retribution by consensus. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: The Harbi did not enter our land for permanent residence but for a need like trade and similar, so he did not become one of the people of our land like the Dhimmi. He only accepted those laws that serve his purpose, which are related to the rights of people and retribution. Hadd for qadhf is among the rights of people, so he is punished for it. As for hadd for zina, it is among the rights of Allah, so it is not enforced against him, unlike the Dhimmi who accepted permanent residence in our land and thus became like a Muslim. Then Muhammad says: The original in the matter of zina is the man's act; the woman is subsidiary. Hence he is called a fornicator and a penetrator, while she is the penetrated and the fornicated. Calling her a fornicator is metaphorical, like 'رضية' meaning 'مَرْضِيَّة' (pleased with). So in the case of a Harbi committing zina with a Dhimmi woman when the hadd falls from the original (the man) due to what we mentioned, it falls from the subsidiary. The reverse is not the case, because the prevention of something in the case of the subsidiary does not necessitate prevention in the case of the original. So the Dhimmi who commits zina with a Harbi woman is hadded, even if the Harbi woman is not hadded. The response from Abu Hanifa: The act of the musta'min Harbi is necessarily zina, and the enablement of an act that is zina necessitates hadd. So when the Dhimmi woman enabled herself for the Harbi's zina, the hadd becomes obligatory on her due to enablement, even if the Dhimmi is not hadded. This clarifies what the author of al-Hidayah mentioned in [2] His statement: 'And this... etc.' is a response to what is said: Conjoining to an attached pronoun of the subject is not permissible according to grammarians, so how did the author commit it? The gist of the response: Non-permissibility is only when there is no separator between the conjoined and the object of conjoining. But when a separator exists, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of the hadd penalty for zina when the perpetrator and victim belong to different dār al-Islām statuses: a Harbī (enemy alien, including musta'min) and a Dhimmī (protected non-Muslim). The commentator explains the text from al-Wiqāyah, which states that according to Imām Muhammad, if a Harbī commits zina with a Dhimmī woman, no hadd is imposed on either party. The author of al-Wiqāyah uses the word 'wa-dhimmiyyatun' (and a Dhimmī woman), which grammatically appears to be conjoined to the hidden pronoun in 'حد' (the subject of the verb 'yuḥaddu'). The commentator defends this grammatical construction, noting that conjoining to an attached pronoun is permissible when a separator (al-fāṣilah) exists, which is the case here. The dispute then unfolds: Abū Yūsuf initially held that only the Dhimmī man is hadded if he commits zina with a Harbiyyah; his later opinion is that both parties are hadded. Abū Yūsuf's reasoning is that the musta'min (a Harbī granted safe passage) has accepted our laws for his stay, just as the Dhimmī has accepted them permanently, so penalties should apply to both. Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad hold that the Harbī enters for temporary purposes (like trade) and only accepts laws concerning transactions and personal rights (like qiṣāṣ and hadd for qadhf), not purely divine rights (like hadd for zina). Muḥammad adds that in zina, the man is the primary actor and the woman is subsidiary, so when the principal's hadd falls, the subsidiary's also falls, but not vice versa. Abū Ḥanīfah responds that the Dhimmī woman's enablement of the act (tamkīn) makes her directly liable, so she is hadded even if the man is not. This clarifies the ruling in al-Hidāyah. The passage concludes with a grammatical defense of the matn's construction.

Key Terms
حربي (ḥarbī): An enemy alien (non-Muslim) who is not under the protection of the Islamic state, including a musta'min (one granted temporary safe passage).
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim living permanently in the Islamic state under a covenant of protection (dhimmah), subject to Islamic laws.
مستأمن (musta'min): A ḥarbī who enters the Islamic state with a safe-conduct (amān) for a temporary purpose, such as trade.
حد الزنا (ḥadd al-zinā): The prescribed corporal punishment for fornication/adultery: 100 lashes for the unmarried, death by stoning for the married.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad, what is the ruling when a Harbī (musta'min) commits zina with a Dhimmī woman?
A) Both are subject to hadd.
B) Neither is subject to hadd.
C) Only the Harbī is subject to hadd.
D) Only the Dhimmī woman is subject to hadd.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position distinguishes between temporary visitors (musta'min) and permanent residents (dhimmi): divine penalties like hadd for zina do not apply to the former, reflecting the scope of laws they have accepted.

Shubha al-Zifaf: No Hadd for Mistaken Wedding Intercourse
Ruling that zifaf creates a shubha averting hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why zifaf creates a shubha that averts hadd, distinguish between shubha al-fi'l and shubha al-mahall, and state the condition for the women's report in this ruling.

Core Idea
A wedding procession creates doubt that blocks hadd — even if the bride is a stranger.

This lesson examines a specific case from Hanafi hudud: a man is presented with a woman as his bride (zifaf), is told she is his wife, and has intercourse, only to discover she is not. The Hanafi school rules that no hadd applies because the situation creates a legal doubt (shubha). We will explore the reasoning, the types of shubha, and the conditions for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the ruling: a man who has intercourse with a foreign woman brought to him as a bride is not subject to hadd; he must pay mahr al-mithl. Note that the ruling applies when the man had no prior knowledge that she was not his wife.
This is the literal first part of the text.
2
Understand the shubha mechanism
The doubt (shubha) arises from the wedding procession (zifaf) itself, not necessarily from the women's report. The zifaf creates a legitimate uncertainty that prevents the application of hadd.
Al-Khaniyyah and Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid emphasize that zifaf alone suffices.
3
Distinguish between the two classifications of shubha
Some Hanafi scholars (al-Zayla'i) classify this as shubha al-fi'l (doubt about the act), while others (al-Fath initially) argue it is shubha al-mahall (doubt about the woman). The preferred view is shubha al-fi'l.
The commentator notes that the truth is shubhat al-ishtibah, i.e., it is a doubt that mixes both.
4
Determine the role of the women's report
The women's report ('she is your wife') is not an absolute condition for hadd to fall; it is only needed when there is no other clear evidence. Mere zifaf can be sufficient.
The commentary refines this from Radd al-Muhtar.
5
Note the consequence
Since hadd is averted, the man must pay the 'uqur (mahr al-mithl) as compensation for the intercourse.
This is stated at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا مَن وَطئ أجنبيةً زُفَتْ إليه ، وقُلْنَ: هي عرسك، وعليه مهرها [١]قوله : ولا من وطئ... الخ ؛ هذه الصورة جعلها الزَّيْلَعِيُّ وغيره من صورِ شبهة الفعل. وذكر في «الفتح»: «إنّ الأوجه أنها من صور شبهة المحل؛ لأن قولهن : هي زوجتك، دليل شرعي مبيح للوطء لقبول قول الواحد في المعاملات ؛ ولذا حلّ وطء من قالت أرسلني مولاي هدية إليك، ثم قال: والحق أنه شبهة اشتباه ؛ أي شبهة الفعل ؛ لأنّ الدليل المعتبر ما يقتضي ثبوت الملك ، لا ما يطلق شرعاً على مُجَرِّد الوطء». انتهى ملخصاً) (٢) قوله : وقلن ؛ إخبار الجمع ليس بقيد، فإن خبر الواحد كان في كل ما يعمل فيه بقول النساء، كذا في «البحر»، ثمّ إخبارُ النِّساءِ غير شرط لسقوط الحد، بل مجرد الزفاف يورث شبهةً على ما يدلّ عليه ما في «الخانية»: رجلٌ زفت إليه غير امرأته ولم یکن رآها قبل ذلك فوطئها، كان عليه المهر ولا حدّ عليه. انتهى. وفي «كافي الحاكم الشهيد»: رجل تزوّج فزفت إليه أخرى فوطئها لا حدّ عليه ولا على قاذفه ، رجل فجر بامرأة ثم قال : حسبتها امرأتي، قال: عليه الحد، وليست هذه كالأولى ؛ لأنّ الزفاف شبهة، ألا ترى أنها إذا جاءت بولد ثبت نسبه، وإن جاءت هذه التي فجر بها بولد لم يثبت نسبه منه. انتهى. والظاهر أنّ إخبار النِّساءِ بكونها عرساً له إنما يكون شرطاً لسقوط الحد إذا لم تقم قرينة ظاهرة تدلّ على كونها عرساً ، كما إذا تزوج امرأة ولم تزف إليه، ثم أدخلت عليه أجنبية ولم يدر أنها هي التي عقد عليها أو غيرها، فأخبرت امرأة بأنها امرأته . فوطئها لا يحدّ ، كذا نقحه في رد المحتار، وهو تنقيح حسن. [٣]قوله : وعليه مهرها ؛ فإن الوطء لا يخلو عن وجوب حدّ أو لزوم عُقر، وهو مهر المثل ، فإذا سقط الحد للشبهة لزم المهر.
And not [subject to hadd] one who had intercourse with a foreign woman who was brought to him as a bride, and they said: 'She is your wife,' and upon him is her mahr. [1] His statement: 'And not one who had intercourse... etc.': This case is considered by al-Zayla'ī and others as instances of shubha al-fi'l (doubt concerning the act). And it is mentioned in al-Fatḥ: 'The more correct view is that it is from shubha al-maḥall (doubt concerning the subject) because their statement 'She is your wife' is a legal evidence permitting intercourse, due to the acceptance of a single person's statement in transactions. Hence, intercourse is lawful for one who says 'My master sent me as a gift to you.' Then he said: 'The truth is that it is shubhat al-ishtibāh, i.e., shubha al-fi'l, because the considered evidence is that which establishes ownership, not that which is legally used for mere intercourse.' End summary. (2) His statement: 'and they said': The report of a group is not a condition, because the report of a single person is sufficient in all matters where women's statements are accepted. As in al-Baḥr, then the women's report is not a condition for the fall of hadd; rather, mere zifāf (the bride's ceremonial delivery) creates a shubha, as indicated by what is in al-Khāniyyah: 'A man was brought a woman other than his wife, and he had not seen her before, and he had intercourse with her: upon him is the mahr and no hadd.' End. And in Kāfī al-Ḥākim al-Shahīd: 'A man married, and another woman was brought to him as a bride, and he had intercourse with her: no hadd upon him and no hadd on his defamer. If a man fornicates with a woman then says 'I thought she was my wife,' he is subject to hadd. This is not like the first because zifāf is a shubha. Do you not see that if she gives birth, his lineage is established; but if that woman with whom he fornicated gives birth, his lineage is not established from him.' End. It appears that the women's report that she is his wife is only a condition for the fall of hadd if there is no apparent evidence indicating that she is his wife. So if a man marries a woman but she is not brought to him, then another woman is brought to him, and he does not know whether she is the one he contracted with or another, and a woman informs him that she is his wife, and he has intercourse with her: he is not hadded. This is how it is refined in Radd al-Muḥtār, and it is a good refinement. [3] His statement: 'and upon him is her mahr': Because intercourse is not without either the obligation of hadd or the liability of 'uqur, which is the mahr al-mithl. So when hadd falls due to shubha, the mahr becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case where the hadd penalty for zina is averted due to doubt (shubha). The ruling is that a man who has intercourse with a woman who is not his wife, but who was brought to him in a wedding procession (zifāf) and others told him she is his wife, is not subject to hadd. Instead, he must pay the mahr al-mithl (customary dower). The commentator explains that there is a difference of opinion among scholars regarding the nature of this shubha. Al-Zayla'ī classifies it as shubha al-fi'l (doubt concerning the act), meaning the man doubted whether the act was permitted. Al-Fatḥ (likely Fatḥ al-Qadīr) argues it is shubha al-maḥall (doubt concerning the subject) because the women's statement that she is his wife provides a legal justification for intercourse, akin to accepting a single witness in transactions. However, the correct view is that it is shubhat al-ishtibāh, i.e., shubha al-fi'l, because the key evidence is that which establishes ownership (milk), not a mere declaration. The commentator then clarifies that the women's report (ikhbār al-nisā') is not a necessary condition for the fall of hadd; rather, the mere fact of the wedding procession (zifāf) creates a shubha that prevents hadd. Supporting this, a ruling from al-Khāniyyah states that if a man is brought a woman other than his wife and has intercourse without knowing, he pays mahr and no hadd. Similarly, in Kāfī al-Ḥākim al-Shahīd: if a man marries and another woman is brought, he is not hadded, and his accuser is not hadded. This contrasts with a fornicator who claims he thought she was his wife—he is hadded because there is no zifāf. The commentator refines that the women's report is only required when there is no other clear evidence that she is his wife. The passage ends by noting that since hadd is averted, the mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory as a substitute.

Key Terms
شبهة الفعل (shubha al-fi'l): A doubt concerning the permissibility of the act itself; the doer is uncertain whether the act is lawful.
شبهة المحل (shubha al-maḥall): A doubt concerning the subject (the woman) – i.e., uncertainty whether she is lawful for him due to a valid reason.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower equivalent to what women of her status would receive, required when no specific dower is agreed.
عقر (ʿuqur): The compensation for intercourse that is not based on a valid marriage; here it is synonymous with mahr al-mithl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a man has intercourse with a woman brought to him as a bride (zifaf) and is told she is his wife, what is the ruling on hadd?
A) He is subject to hadd because she is a foreign woman.
B) He is not subject to hadd, but must pay mahr al-mithl.
C) He is subject to hadd only if the women's report is false.
D) He is not subject to hadd and owes no mahr.

Key Takeaway
The zifaf (bridal procession) creates a legal doubt that blocks hadd, and the man must compensate with mahr al-mithl.

Dropping of Hadd and Obligation of Mahr in Mistaken Intercourse
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd and mahr when a man has intercourse with a woman mistakenly brought as his bride, and explain why shubha precludes the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Shubha from zufaf drops the hadd but does not void the obligation of mahr.

In Hanafi criminal law, the fixed penalty (hadd) for zina is averted by any plausible legal doubt (shubha). One classic case is when a woman is brought to a man as his bride (zufaf) and he has intercourse believing she is his wife. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its basis in the hadith 'Idra'u al-hududa bi al-shubuhat', and the distinction between this scenario and clear zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is intercourse with an unrelated woman who was brought to the man as his bride (mazfufa). The ruling: no hadd penalty, but mahr (or 'uqr) is wajib.
2
Identify the shubha and its source
The shubha arises from the zufaf (the woman being brought) and possibly the women's report that she is his wife. This creates a plausible doubt that the woman is his lawful wife.
The text clarifies that even without any report, the zufaf alone creates shubha.
3
Distinguish between two views on the nature of shubha
Al-Zayla'i views it as shubhat al-fi'l (doubt in the act – the man did not intend zina). The author of al-Fath views it as shubhat al-mahall (doubt in the subject – the woman's claim gives legal basis). Both agree on the ruling.
This intra-Hanafi difference does not affect the fatwa.
4
State the obligation of mahr
Intercourse without a valid marriage either incurs hadd or obligates 'uqr (compensation). Since shubha drops the hadd, the 'uqr, which is mahr al-mithl, becomes wajib.
5
Distinguish from clear zina
The case is contrasted with a man who commits zina and later claims ignorance – there, hadd applies because there is no shubha like zufaf. The zufaf creates a real ambiguity that precludes the hadd.
The proof: if a child is born from the zufaf case, lineage is established; in zina, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا مَن وَطئ أجنبيةً زُفَتْ إليه ، وقُلْنَ: هي عرسك، وعليه مهرها [١]قوله : ولا من وطئ... الخ ؛ ؛ هذه الصورة جعلها الزَّيْلَعِيُّ وغيره من صورِ شبهة الفعل. وذكر في «الفتح»: «إنّ الأوجه أنها من صور شبهة المحل؛ لأن قولهن : هي زوجتك، دليل شرعي مبيح للوطء لقبول قول الواحد في المعاملات ؛ ولذا حلّ وطء من قالت أرسلني مولاي هدية إليك، ثم قال: والحق أنه شبهة اشتباه ؛ أي شبهة الفعل ؛ لأنّ الدليل المعتبر ما يقتضي ثبوت الملك ، لا ما يطلق شرعاً على مُجَرِّد الوطء». انتهى ملخصاً) (٢) قوله : وقلن ؛ إخبار الجمع ليس بقيد، فإن خبر الواحد كان في كل ما يعمل فيه بقول النساء، كذا في «البحر»، ثمّ إخبارُ النِّساءِ غير شرط لسقوط الحد، بل مجرد الزفاف يورث شبهةً على ما يدلّ عليه ما في «الخانية»: رجلٌ زفت إليه غير امرأته ولم یکن رآها قبل ذلك فوطئها، كان عليه المهر ولا حدّ عليه. انتهى. وفي «كافي الحاكم الشهيد»: رجل تزوّج فزفت إليه أخرى فوطئها لا حدّ عليه ولا على قاذفه ، رجل فجر بامرأة ثم قال : حسبتها امرأتي، قال: عليه الحد، وليست هذه كالأولى ؛ لأنّ الزفاف شبهة، ألا ترى أنها إذا جاءت بولد ثبت نسبه، وإن جاءت هذه التي فجر بها بولد لم يثبت نسبه منه. انتهى. والظاهر أنّ إخبار النِّساءِ بكونها عرساً له إنما يكون شرطاً لسقوط الحد إذا لم تقم قرينة ظاهرة تدلّ على كونها عرساً ، كما إذا تزوج امرأة ولم تزف إليه، ثم أدخلت عليه أجنبية ولم يدر أنها هي التي عقد عليها أو غيرها، فأخبرت امرأة بأنها امرأته . فوطئها لا يحدّ ، كذا نقحه في رد المحتار، وهو تنقيح حسن. [٣]قوله : وعليه مهرها ؛ فإن الوطء لا يخلو عن وجوب حدّ أو لزوم عُقر، وهو مهر المثل ، فإذا سقط الحد للشبهة لزم المهر.
And not the one who had intercourse with an unrelated woman who was brought to him, and they (women) said: 'She is your wife', and upon him is her mahr. His saying: 'and not the one who had intercourse...' etc. This case was considered by al-Zayla'i and others as cases of shubha in the act. And it is mentioned in al-Fath: 'The more correct view is that it is from cases of shubha in the subject, because their saying 'She is your wife' is a legal evidence permitting intercourse due to accepting the word of a single person in transactions; and therefore intercourse is permissible for the one who says: My master sent me to you as a gift. Then he said: The truth is that it is a shubha of confusion, i.e., shubha in the act, because the considered evidence is that which necessitates establishment of ownership, not what is legally applied to mere intercourse.' End summary. (2) His saying: 'and they said' — the report of the plural is not a condition, for the report of a single person was in everything in which the word of women is acted upon, as in al-Bahr. Then the report of women is not a condition for dropping the hadd; rather, mere bringing (zufaf) causes a shubha, as indicated by what is in al-Khaniyyah: 'A man to whom a woman other than his wife was brought and he had not seen her before and he had intercourse with her, upon him is the mahr and no hadd.' End. And in Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid: 'A man married, and another (woman) was brought to him, and he had intercourse with her, no hadd upon him and no hadd upon the one who slanders him. A man committed zina with a woman then said: I thought she was my wife. He said: Upon him is the hadd, and this is not like the first case because the bringing (zufaf) is a shubha. Do you not see that if she comes with a child, his lineage is established, while if this woman with whom he committed zina comes with a child, his lineage is not established from him.' End. And it appears that the women's report that she is his wife is only a condition for dropping the hadd if there is no apparent evidence indicating that she is his wife, such as if he married a woman and she was not brought to him, then an unrelated woman was brought to him and he did not know whether she was the one he married or another, then a woman informed him that she is his wife, and he had intercourse with her, he is not subjected to hadd. So was refined in Radd al-Muhtar, and it is a good refinement. His saying: 'and upon him is her mahr' — for intercourse does not avoid either obligation of hadd or (obligation of) 'uqr, which is the mahr al-mithl; so when the hadd is dropped due to shubha, the mahr becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage examines the case of a man who unknowingly has intercourse with a woman brought to him as his bride (the 'zufaf' scenario). The ruling is that the hadd penalty for zina is dropped because of shubha (legal doubt), but he must pay the mahr (dower). The text discusses the nature of the shubha: some scholars (like al-Zayla'i) consider it shubhat al-fi'l (doubt in the act) because the man didn't intend zina, while others (like the author of al-Fath) consider it shubhat al-mahall (doubt in the object) because the woman's claim of being his wife provides a legal basis for permissibility. The passage also clarifies that mere zufaf (the woman being brought) is itself a shubha, even without witnesses. It distinguishes this case from clear zina where the man claims ignorance, because the zufaf creates a real ambiguity. Finally, the payment of mahr is explained: since the hadd is dropped, the 'uqr (compensation for intercourse) becomes due, which is the mahr al-mithl (equivalent dower). The Hanafi position is that shubha averts hadd but does not make the act halal; the perpetrator is still liable for mahr and ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in some cases.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that arises from a plausible scenario, causing the hadd penalty to be averted. Here, the zufaf creates a shubha that the woman might be his wife.
زفاف (zufaf): The act of bringing the bride to the groom's house; in this context, the bride being brought to the man creates a strong presumption that she is his wife.
مهر (mahr): The dower paid to the woman; in this case, the man must pay the mahr al-mithl (equivalent dower) because he has had intercourse with her, even though no valid marriage exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man has intercourse with a woman who was mistakenly brought to him as a bride (zuffat ilayhi), what is the ruling?
A) He is subject to the hadd penalty for zina.
B) He is not subject to hadd but must pay the mahr.
C) He is not subject to hadd and does not owe mahr.
D) He is subject to ta'zir but not hadd.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the principle 'Idra'u al-hududa bi al-shubuhat' to drop the hadd penalty when a plausible doubt (shubha) exists, such as when a woman is mistakenly brought as a bride, but the man must still pay the mahr as compensation for the intercourse.

Intercourse with Mahram After Marriage: No Hadd but Ta'zir
Defends Abu Hanifa's ruling that hadd is dropped for intercourse with a mahram after a marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse with a mahram after an invalid marriage and explain why the hadd is averted but ta'zir is applied, citing the principle of shubha.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by shubha, not permissibility; ta'zir remains.

The Hanafi school is sometimes criticized for ruling that a man who marries his mahram and has intercourse is not subject to the hadd penalty. This passage defends Abu Hanifa's position, clarifying that the hadd is dropped due to the doubt (shubha) created by the marriage contract, but the act is still sinful and punishable by ta'zir. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the remedy of hadd and the moral status of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is intercourse with a mahram (blood relative) after an invalid marriage contract. The ruling is that the hadd penalty for zina is not applied.
The marriage is invalid because marriage to a mahram is permanently prohibited.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the hadith 'Idra'u al-hududa bi al-shubuhat' (Avert the hadd penalties by doubts). The marriage contract creates a shubha that the intercourse might be within a valid marriage, so the hadd is averted.
The hadith is widely transmitted and considered authoritative.
3
Understand the principle of shubha
Shubha (doubt) can avert the hadd even if the act is haram. Here, the invalid marriage contract creates a doubt about the legal status of the intercourse, so the fixed penalty is not applied.
This is a well-established principle in Hanafi fiqh, not an innovation.
4
Recognize that ta'zir is applied
Although hadd is dropped, the perpetrator is still sinful and subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The judge may impose flogging or another deterrent penalty.
Ta'zir is explicitly mentioned in al-Hidayah and other works.
5
Address the common misconception
Critics argue that dropping hadd implies the act is halal. This is false; the act remains haram and sinful. Dropping hadd is a separate legal outcome due to shubha, not a statement of permissibility.
The author emphasizes that permissibility (halal) is not the same as averting punishment.

Source Text

And the mahram whom he married) is a conjunction to his saying: 'an unrelated woman' (ajnabiyyah). His saying: 'And the mahram whom he married' is a conjunction to his saying: 'an unrelated woman'; i.e., he is not subjected to hadd who had intercourse with a mahram among his maharim, like mother, maternal aunt, sister, after he married her. And this matter has been attacked by a group of the Shi'a and those who imitate them from the Ahl al-Sunnah against Abu Hanifa, and some of them publicized that intercourse with maharim and marrying them is halal according to the Hanafis. And among them are those who said that he contradicted authentic hadiths therein. So they attacked the great Imam Abu Hanifa in this matter with an attack that went beyond bounds, and they refuted him with the ugliest refutation without understanding his principles and reflecting on his proofs. And I have written a treatise on this matter which I named 'Al-Qawl al-Jazim bi Suqut al-Hadd bi Nikah al-Mahrim', in which I strengthened his madhhab with rational and transmitted proofs, supported his statements with principles, repelled the doubts of the doubters in a way that breaks the backs of the obstinate, and mentioned what pertains to it of disagreement and branch rulings along with answers to the attacks and wounds. If you wish to see it, refer to it, for it is precious in its field, with no equal in its research. And we will mention a sample of the famous attacks among the laity along with the answer to them as approved by the scholars: So know that among their attacks is: 1. That dropping the hadd for intercourse with maharim after marrying them necessitates the permissibility of this heinous act and the absence of sin and blame on the perpetrator of this ugly deed. This is a false attack according to every virtuous person, for dropping the hadd is one thing, and permissibility of the act is another thing; and the dropping of hadd does not imply its permissibility. How? Because the hadd is averted by shubuhat (doubts) as established by the reports and traditions transmitted by trustworthy narrators. Therefore, Abu Hanifa also ruled that the one who has intercourse with maharim after marrying them is subject to ta'zir and painful punishment, as stated in al-Hidayah and other works.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling that a man who marries a mahram (blood relative) and then has intercourse with her is not subject to the hadd penalty for zina. This is because the marriage contract, though invalid (since marriage to a mahram is prohibited), creates a shubha (doubt) that averts the hadd. However, this does not mean the act is halal. Abu Hanifa explicitly states that such a person is to be punished with ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and is sinful. The author vigorously defends Abu Hanifa against critics who claim that this ruling implies permissibility. He explains that the hadd is dropped due to the principle 'Idra'u al-hududa bi al-shubuhat' (Avert the hadd penalties by doubts), and that dropping the hadd is not equivalent to making the act lawful. The author also mentions his own treatise on the subject for further details. The Hanafi position is that while the hadd is averted, the perpetrator is still subject to ta'zir, emphasizing that the act remains sinful.

Key Terms
محرمة (mahram): A female blood relative whom one is permanently prohibited from marrying, such as mother, sister, daughter, aunt, etc. In this context, the woman is a mahram, but the ruler marries her (an invalid marriage) and then has intercourse.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment administered by the judge for sins or offenses that do not have a fixed hadd penalty. In this case, the perpetrator is subject to ta'zir, which may include flogging or other deterrent punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The hadd is dropped due to shubha, but ta'zir is imposed; the act remains sinful.

Defense of Abu Hanifa on Hadd for Wati' al-Mahram after Nikah
Inter-madhhab dispute defending Hanafi view on hadd falling due to shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that hadd falls due to shubha for intercourse with a mahram after marriage, distinguish it from a claim of permissibility, and evaluate the opposing arguments and Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
Hadd falls due to shubha, but the act remains haram and subject to ta'zir.

This passage is a vigorous defense of Imam Abu Hanifa against accusations that he considered intercourse with mahrams permissible. The author systematically refutes three objections: that dropping hadd implies halal, that it contradicts the Quran, and that it contradicts authentic hadith. The key distinction is between the fall of a fixed punishment (hadd) and the ruling on the act itself. By the end, you will understand how the principle 'hudud are averted by doubts' operates in this controversial case and why the Hanafi school applies ta'zir instead of hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the controversy
State the Hanafi position: hadd is not applied for intercourse with a mahram after a marriage contract. Note that opponents claim this implies permissibility. This sets up the three criticisms.
2
Examine the first criticism and response
Critics say falling of hadd implies the act is halal. The response distinguishes between the fall of hadd (due to shubha) and the legal ruling on the act (haram). Ta'zir is still obligatory.
The analogy with drinking blood and eating pork illustrates that no hadd does not mean no punishment.
3
Analyze the second criticism
Critics claim contradiction with the Quranic prohibition of marrying mahrams and command of hadd for zina. Response: Abu Hanifa affirms the prohibition and the principle of hadd, but the marriage contract creates a shubha that averts the specific hadd penalty.
4
Evaluate the third criticism
Critics cite hadiths commanding death for intercourse with a mahram. The response argues that these hadiths are either weak or overridden by the stronger, established principle that hudud are averted by doubts.
The author lists sources but does not fully analyze each chain; the emphasis is on the primacy of the shubha principle.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, the offender is given ta'zir (discretionary punishment) rather than hadd (stoning or flogging). In schools that reject the shubha, the offender may face hadd or execution.
